Now, the problem: Determine all possible values of a1 such that the infinite sequence exists (each term has at least 3 proper divisors). We have found that if a1 equals any fixed point, then the sequence is constant and infinite. Also, there might be other a1 that eventually become a fixed point or cycle that never hits a number with <3 proper divisors. We need to examine whether any a1 not a fixed point can lead to an infinite sequence. Since the recurrence f(N) is defined for N with at least 3 proper divisors, we need that all a_n have at least 3 proper divisors. If at any point we get a number with <3 proper divisors, the sequence fails. So we need to determine all a1 for which the iterates never hit a number with <3 proper divisors. This is a kind of "trapping" set.

We can try to analyze the function f(N). Note that for N>1, the sum of the three largest proper divisors is less than N? Not always. For N=6, f=6 equals N. For N=18, f=18 equals N. So we need to see whether starting from a given N, the sequence can be infinite without hitting a number with <3 proper divisors.

Observations: If N is a fixed point, it's fine. If N is not a fixed point, then either f(N) > N or f(N) < N. Could the sequence be unbounded? Let's try to analyze the function f(N) more systematically. Define the proper divisors set. Let s(N) = sum of three largest proper divisors. Write N = p1^{e1} p2^{e2} ... pk^{ek} with p1 < p2 < ... < pk. Then the three smallest divisors >1 are: d1 = p1, d2 = min(p1^2, p2) if e1>=2 else p2 (if k>=2), and d3 = third smallest among p1^3, p1p2, p2^2, etc., but we can list possibilities.

But maybe there is a known result: The sum of the three largest proper divisors of N is equal to N * (1/p1 + 1/p2 + 1/p1^2?) Not exactly.

Alternatively, note that the three largest proper divisors are N/d1, N/d2, N/d3, where d1,d2,d3 are the three smallest divisors >1. So f(N) = N(1/d1+1/d2+1/d3). This holds if N has at least 3 divisors >1. If N has exactly 2 divisors >1, then f(N) = N/d1+N/d2+1 = N(1/d1+1/d2)+1, and we can think of that as N(1/d1+1/d2+1/N) but 1/N is not a reciprocal of an integer >1.

Now, the condition that each a_n has at least 3 proper divisors is that a_n is not prime and not p^2. So we need to avoid numbers that are prime or prime squares.

So the only possible a1 are those that eventually become a fixed point. And we have found some families: fixed points themselves, and numbers that map to fixed points via f. But are there numbers that map to fixed points in more than one step? For instance, 72->78 fixed, so one step. 216->234 fixed, one step. 648->702 fixed, one step. So these are numbers N such that f(N) is a fixed point. Could there be numbers that map to such numbers, and then to fixed points? For instance, if we find M such that f(M) = N and f(N) = fixed, then M would also work. So the basin of attraction might be infinite. We need to determine all numbers that eventually reach a fixed point. This is a directed graph; we need to find all numbers whose forward orbit stays in T. This is like the "non-escaping" set.

Given the problem is an olympiad problem, the answer is likely to be that a1 must be a multiple of 6, but also not divisible by 4? But we have counterexamples: 72 is divisible by 4 and works. However, maybe 72 does not have at least three proper divisors? 72 has 11 proper divisors, so yes. So why would 72 be invalid? Let's re-read the problem: "A proper divisor of a positive integer N is a positive divisor of N other than N itself. The infinite sequence a1, a2, ... consists of positive integers, each of which has at least three proper divisors. For each n ≥ 1, the integer a_{n+1} is the sum of the three largest proper divisors of a_n. Determine all possible values of a1." So a1 must be such that there exists an infinite sequence satisfying the conditions. For a1=72, we can define a2=78, a3=78,... and each term has at least three proper divisors. So 72 is valid. So the answer includes 72. But maybe we need to consider that the sequence must be infinite, and also each term must have at least three proper divisors. That's fine. So the set is not just fixed points.

Maybe the problem expects that a1 must be a multiple of 6, and also not divisible by 4? But then 72 would be excluded, but we found it works. Let's double-check the definition: "proper divisor" is a positive divisor of N other than N itself. So for 78, proper divisors: 1,2,3,6,13,26,39. The three largest are 39,26,13 sum=78. So a3=78. So the sequence from 72 is 72,78,78,... All terms have at least three proper divisors. So 72 is valid. So maybe the answer is that a1 must be a multiple of 6? But 24 is a multiple of 6 and fails. So not that. Possibly the answer is that a1 must be a multiple of 6 that is not divisible by 5? But 24 is not divisible by 5 and fails. So no.

Let's think differently. Maybe we can prove that if a1 is not a fixed point, then the sequence eventually becomes strictly increasing and unbounded, and then we might get a prime or prime square? But we saw 72->78 (increase), then constant. So not increasing after that. But maybe we can prove that if a1 is not a fixed point, then the sequence must eventually reach a number with <3 proper divisors, except for some special numbers that are preimages of fixed points. And those preimages might themselves be only of a certain form. Perhaps we can characterize all numbers that map to a fixed point. That is, find all N such that f(N) is a fixed point. And then those N also work. And then numbers that map to such N also work, etc. So the set of all possible a1 is the set of all numbers that eventually reach a fixed point under f.

But is it possible that there is a cycle of length >1? Let's try to search for 2-cycles systematically. Suppose N and M are in T, with f(N)=M, f(M)=N, N≠M. Then as argued, if both have at least 3 divisors >1, then we have f(N)=N(1/d1+1/d2+1/d3)=M, f(M)=M(1/e1+1/e2+1/e3)=N. Then multiplying gives (1/d1+1/d2+1/d3)(1/e1+1/e2+1/e3)=1. Since each sum >1 (because for numbers with at least 3 divisors >1, the sum of reciprocals of three smallest divisors is at least 1/2+1/3+1/4=13/12>1), the product cannot be 1 unless each sum is exactly 1? But product of two numbers >1 cannot be 1. So it's impossible for both to have at least 3 divisors >1. So at least one of them has exactly 2 divisors >1. That means one of N or M is either p^3 or p*q. So suppose N has exactly 2 divisors >1. Then N is either p^3 or p*q. Then f(N)=N/p+N/q+1. For N=p^3, f(N)=p^2+p+1. For N=p*q, f(N)=p+q+1. We need M = f(N). Then we need f(M)=N. Now, M might have at least 3 divisors >1 or exactly 2. Let's explore possibilities.

Case: N = p*q (distinct primes). Then M = p+q+1. We need f(M)=pq. Since M may have various divisors. For pq to be a fixed point? Actually we need f(M)=N. N is not necessarily a fixed point. For N=6, p=2,q=3 gives M=2+3+1=6, so f(6)=6, which is a fixed point, so that's a 1-cycle. For N=10 (2*5), M=2+5+1=8. Then f(8)=? 8 has proper divisors 1,2,4; sum=7, not 10. So no 2-cycle. For N=15 (3*5), M=3+5+1=9. f(9)=? 9 has proper divisors 1,3 (two), so not allowed. So no. For N=21 (3*7), M=3+7+1=11 prime, fails. So no.

What about a 2-cycle where one of them has exactly 2 divisors >1 and the other has at least 3? Then we have M = f(N) with N having exactly 2 divisors >1. Then we need f(M)=N. For N=p^3, M=p^2+p+1. Thus no 2-cycles exist. Similarly, longer cycles likely impossible because product of sums of reciprocals would be 1, but each sum >1 if the number has at least 3 divisors >1; if some numbers have exactly 2 divisors >1, then the mapping is not simply reciprocal sum. Possibly one could have a cycle that involves numbers with exactly 2 divisors >1, but we saw that numbers of the form p^3 or p*q map to numbers that are not p^3 or p*q in general. Could there be a cycle of length 3? Unlikely. So it seems the only possible infinite sequences are those that eventually become constant at a fixed point. So the set of possible a1 is exactly the set of numbers that eventually map to a fixed point under f, without hitting a prime or prime square. This is the "basin of attraction" of the set of fixed points.

Now, we need to characterize all such numbers. This might be done by showing that if a1 is not a fixed point, then its forward orbit is strictly increasing until it hits a fixed point, and that the only numbers that map to a fixed point are those of the form 8*3^k for k≥2, and numbers that map to those, etc. But we need a proof.

Maybe there is a known result: For N>1, the sum of the three largest proper divisors is N if and only if N is of the form 2*3^k*m where m is product of primes >5? Actually we derived that if N has at least 3 divisors >1, then f(N)=N iff N is divisible by 6, v2(N)=1, and 5∤N. That is the fixed point set. So let's denote F = {N: N is a multiple of 6, v2(N)=1, 5∤N}. For N=6, it's also in F? 6=2*3, v2=1, 5∤6, yes. So F is exactly the set of fixed points.

Now, we need to find all N such that f(N) ∈ F, and also such that the intermediate iterates are in T (i.e., they have at least 3 proper divisors). For N that are not in F, but f(N) is in F, we have a 1-step preimage. Then numbers that map to such N also work, etc. So the set of all possible a1 is the set of all numbers whose forward orbit under f eventually lands in F, and never leaves T before that. But note that if we ever hit a number not in T (prime or p^2), the sequence fails. So we need that all numbers in the orbit are in T. In particular, if we have a preimage of a fixed point, we need to ensure that the fixed point itself is in T (it is, because it has at least 3 proper divisors). And also that the preimage is in T. So we need to characterize the preimages of F under f.

This is a kind of "preimage" problem: Given a fixed point M ∈ F, find all N such that f(N)=M. Since f(N) is defined for N with at least 3 proper divisors, but we also need N ∈ T. So we need to solve f(N)=M for N ∈ T. And then recursively, preimages of those preimages, etc.

Given the problem is from an olympiad, the answer is likely to be that a1 must be a multiple of 6, but also not divisible by 4 or 5? Thus it seems that the only numbers that work are those that are either fixed points (i.e., multiples of 6 with v2=1 and 5∤N) or numbers that are "preimages" of fixed points via f, i.e., numbers N such that f(N) is a fixed point. And we have found that 8*3^k for k≥2 are such preimages. Are there any other preimages? Let's try to find all N such that f(N) = M for a given fixed point M. Since M is fixed, we have f(M)=M. So we need to solve f(N)=M. For N with at least 3 divisors >1, we have f(N)=N/d1+N/d2+N/d3. This is a linear equation in N. For N with exactly 2 divisors >1, we have f(N)=N/d1+N/d2+1. So we can try to find all N.

Let's denote the three smallest divisors >1 for N (if they exist). But maybe we can characterize the preimages by analyzing the possible values of d1,d2,d3. Since M is fixed, we have M = N/d1+N/d2+N/d3. Also note that d1,d2,d3 are divisors of N, and d1<d2<d3. Also d1≥2, d2≥3, d3≥? In particular, N/d1 is a proper divisor of N, and N/d2, N/d3 are proper divisors as well. So N must be such that its three largest proper divisors sum to M. Since M is a fixed point, it is known that M is a multiple of 6, v2(M)=1, 5∤M. So M = 2 * 3^k * r where r is product of primes >5? Actually v2=1, so M=2 * (odd). Also 5∤M, so M not divisible by 5. Also M divisible by 3? Not necessarily: M=2*13=26? But 26=2*13, v2=1,5∤26, but is 26 fixed? f(26)=? 26=2*13, divisors >1:2,13, so f=13+2+1=16, not 26. So 26 is not fixed. So for fixed points, we also need that 3 divides M? Because from the triple (2,3,6) condition, we need that 2,3,6 divide M. So M must be divisible by 6. So M divisible by 3. So M = 2*3^k * s where s is product of primes >5? Actually s can be 1 or product of primes >5? But also need that v2(M)=1 and 5∤M. So M is even, divisible by 3, not by 4 or 5. So M = 2 * 3^k * t, where t is odd and not divisible by 5, and also t must be such that no divisor between 3 and 6 exists? Actually if t>1, then the divisors >1 of M include 2,3,6, and possibly other small primes. But as long as t does not contain any prime less than 6? Actually if t contains 7, then divisors >1 include 2,3,6,7, etc. The three smallest are 2,3,6 (since 7>6). So that's fine. So M can be any number of the form 2*3^k * t where t is product of primes >5 (or 1) and also 5∤t. So M is a multiple of 6.

Now, we need to find N such that f(N)=M. Since M is fixed, we have f(M)=M. So the orbit from N goes to M then constant. So N works.

Now, we need to find all such N. Let's attempt to characterize N by its smallest divisors. Suppose N has at least 3 divisors >1. Then let d1,d2,d3 be the three smallest divisors >1. Then f(N)=N/d1+N/d2+N/d3 = M. Also note that N/d1, N/d2, N/d3 are divisors of N that are greater than or equal to N/d3? Actually since d1<d2<d3, we have N/d1 > N/d2 > N/d3. And N/d3 is a proper divisor. Also note that N/d3 is at least something. Since d3 is the third smallest divisor >1, we have d3≥? For N not divisible by 4? Let's not assume.

We can also consider the possibility that N has exactly 2 divisors >1. Then f(N)=N/d1+N/d2+1 = M. Then N/d1+N/d2 = M-1. Since d1 and d2 are the two smallest divisors >1, they are either p and p^2 for p^3, or p and q for p*q. Then N/d1+N/d2 = either p^2+p or p+q. So M-1 = p^2+p or p+q. Since M is a fixed point, we can try to find p,q such that M = p^2+p+1 or M = p+q+1. For p=2,q=3, we get M=6, which is fixed. For p=2,q=5, M=8, but 8 is not fixed. For p=2,q=7, M=10, not fixed. For p=3,q=5, M=9, but 9 is not fixed (and also N=15? Actually N=15 gives M=9, but 9 is not fixed). So only possibility from the two-divisor case is N=6, which is itself a fixed point. So no new preimages from that case.

Thus we need to consider N with at least 3 divisors >1. Then we have f(N)=N(1/d1+1/d2+1/d3)=M. Since M is a fixed point, we have M = N * S, where S = 1/d1+1/d2+1/d3 is a rational number. Also note that d1,d2,d3 are divisors of N. Since d1 is the smallest divisor >1, it must be a prime. Let d1 = p1. Then d2 is either p1^2 if p1^2 divides N, or the second smallest prime factor p2 if p2 < p1^2. Similarly d3. So S is of the form 1/p1 + 1/p2 + 1/p3? Not exactly, because d2 might be p1^2, d3 might be p1^3 or p1p2, etc. But we can enumerate possibilities.

Since M is a fixed point, it is known that M has the form 2*3^k * t, with t odd and not divisible by 5. Also M is divisible by 6. So M is even and divisible by 3. That suggests that N might also have some structure.

Let's check the original problem statement again: "A proper divisor of a positive integer N is a positive divisor of N other than N itself. The infinite sequence a1, a2, ... consists of positive integers, each of which has at least three proper divisors. For each n ≥ 1, the integer a_{n+1} is the sum of the three largest proper divisors of a_n. Determine all possible values of a1." So the sequence is infinite, and each term has at least three proper divisors. So if a1=72, we can define the sequence as 72,78,78,... and each term has at least three proper divisors. Now, we analyze the iteration.

Thus, a is odd. Now, we show that b ≥ (a-1)/2+1 and 5∤u are necessary.

Let's write the proof elegantly.

We'll start:

Let f(N) denote the sum of the three largest proper divisors of N. For N with at least three proper divisors, f(N) is defined.

We'll prove that the only possible a1 are those numbers that are divisible by 6, not divisible by 5, and satisfy that if a1 = 2^a 3^b t with t odd and not divisible by 3, then a is odd and b ≥ (a-1)/2+1.

We'll do it in steps.

First, we show that a1 must be a multiple of 6. Suppose a1 is not a multiple of 6. Then either a1 is odd or a1 is even but not divisible by 3. In either case, we can show that f(a1) < a1. Indeed, if a1 is odd, then the smallest divisor >1 is an odd prime p≥3. The three largest proper divisors correspond to the three smallest divisors d1<d2<d3, with d1≥3. Then 1/d1+1/d2+1/d3 ≤ 1/3+1/5+1/7 < 1, so f(a1) < a1. If a1 is even but not divisible by 3, then the smallest divisor is 2. The three smallest divisors are at least 2,3,4 (if 4 divides) or 2,3,5, etc. In any case, the sum of reciprocals is at most 1/2+1/4+1/5 = 19/20 < 1, so f(a1) < a1. Moreover, if a1 is a prime or a square of a prime, then it has fewer than three proper divisors, so the sequence cannot start. Hence, a1 must be divisible by 6. So a1 = 6k.

Write a1 = 2^a 3^b u, where a≥1, b≥1, u is odd and not divisible by 3.

Now, consider the iteration.

Case a = 1. Then the three smallest divisors >1 are 2,3,6 if 5 ∤ u, giving f(a1) = a1, a fixed point. If 5 | u, then the third smallest divisor is 5, so f(a1) = (31/30)a1, which is odd and divisible by 3. Then as argued, any odd number not a multiple of 6 satisfies f(M) < M, so the sequence from that odd number strictly decreases until it reaches a prime or a square of a prime, which would break the condition. Therefore, for an infinite sequence, we must have a=1 and 5 ∤ u.

Case a ≥ 2. Then a1 is divisible by 4, so the three smallest divisors are 2,3,4. Hence, f(a1) = (13/12)a1. Also, v2(f(a1)) = a-2, v3(f(a1)) = b-1.

We claim that if a is even, then the sequence cannot be infinite. Suppose a is even and a≥2. Then a-2 is even. Iterating, as long as v2 ≥ 2 and v3 ≥ 1, we have v2 even and v3 decreasing. After t steps, we have v2 = a-2t (even), v3 = b-t. When t = b, we get a number N_b = 13^b 2^{a-2b} u. Since a is even, a-2b is even. If a-2b > 0, then N_b is even and not divisible by 3; if a-2b = 0, then N_b is odd and not divisible by 3. In either case, the number is not a multiple of 6, so we are back in the situation where f(N) < N. Hence, the sequence would strictly decrease from that point and eventually hit a prime or a square of a prime, contradiction. Therefore, a must be odd.

Now, assume a is odd and a ≥ 3. Then we have v2(a1)=a odd ≥3. The iteration gives numbers of the form N_t = 13^t 2^{a-2t} 3^{b-t} u for t = 0,1,2,... . As long as a-2t ≥ 2 and b-t ≥ 1, we have f(N_t) = N_{t+1} and the sequence increases. The process stops when either a-2t = 1 or b-t = 0. We need to ensure that the sequence never reaches a number that is not a multiple of 6 and that would cause a decrease. For the sequence to be infinite, the orbit must stay within numbers that have at least three proper divisors. In particular, we must never encounter a number that is even but not divisible by 3 (since such numbers are not multiples of 6 and have f(N) < N, leading to a decreasing path that eventually fails). Therefore, the process must reach a state where v2 = 1 and v3 ≥ 1, and then the number becomes a fixed point. This requires that b - t ≥ 1 when a-2t = 1. Hence, we need b ≥ t+1 with t = (a-1)/2. Thus, b ≥ (a-1)/2 + 1. Also, when a-2t = 1, the number is 2 * 3^{b-t} * 13^t * u. For it to be a fixed point, we need that the third smallest divisor is 6, which requires that 5 does not divide u (otherwise d3=5). Hence, 5 ∤ u.

Conversely, if a is odd, b ≥ (a-1)/2+1, and 5 ∤ u, then we can show that the sequence is infinite: for t = 0,1,..., (a-1)/2, N_t = 13^t 2^{a-2t} 3^{b-t} u is divisible by 2 and 3, so f(N_t) is defined. Moreover, for t < (a-1)/2, we have a-2t ≥ 2, so the triple is (2,3,4) and f(N_t)=N_{t+1}. When t = (a-1)/2, we get N = 2 * 3^{b-(a-1)/2} * 13^{(a-1)/2} * u, with v3 = b-(a-1)/2 ≥ 1 and v2=1, and since 5 ∤ u, the three smallest divisors are 2,3,6, so f(N)=N. Thus, the sequence becomes constant at this fixed point. All terms have at least three proper divisors (since they are multiples of 6), so the sequence is infinite.

Therefore, the possible values of a1 are precisely the positive integers N such that N is not a prime, not a square of a prime, and when we write N = 2^a 3^b t with t odd and not divisible by 3, we have a odd, b ≥ (a-1)/2+1, and 5 ∤ t.

We can also state: a1 must be divisible by 6, not divisible by 5, and the exponent of 2 in a1 must be odd, and the exponent of 3 must be at least (exponent of 2 - 1)/2 + 1.

Now, we can present the final answer in a box.

We'll write:

\boxed{\text{All positive integers } N \text{ that are not prime, not a square of a prime, and such that if } N = 2^a 3^b t \text{ with } t \text{ odd and } 3 \nmid t, \text{ then } a \text{ is odd, } b \geq \frac{a-1}{2}+1, \text{ and } 5 \nmid t.}

Alternatively, we can write: \boxed{a_1 \in \{2^a 3^b t \mid a \text{ odd}, b \ge \frac{a-1}{2}+1, t \text{ odd}, 3 \nmid t, 5 \nmid t\}}.

We'll write the solution in a clear, step-by-step proof.

Let's craft the final answer.</think><solution>
Let \(f(N)\) denote the sum of the three largest proper divisors of a positive integer \(N\).  
For \(N\) with at least three proper divisors the function is defined.

We determine all possible values of \(a_1\) such that the infinite sequence
\(a_1,a_2,\dots\) consists of positive integers each having at least three proper divisors
and satisfies \(a_{n+1}=f(a_n)\) for all \(n\ge 1\).

---

### 1.  \(a_1\) must be a multiple of \(6\)

If \(a_1\) is not divisible by \(6\) then either \(a_1\) is odd or \(a_1\) is even but not divisible by \(3\).
In both cases we show that \(f(a_1)<a_1\) and the sequence strictly decreases.  

*If \(a_1\) is odd*: its smallest divisor \(>1\) is an odd prime \(p\ge3\).  
The three largest proper divisors correspond to the three smallest divisors \(d_1<d_2<d_3\) with \(d_1\ge3\).  
Then  
\[
\frac1{d_1}+\frac1{d_2}+\frac1{d_3}\le\frac13+\frac15+\frac17<\frac{71}{105}<1,
\]
so \(f(a_1)<a_1\).  

*If \(a_1\) is even but not divisible by \(3\)*: the smallest divisor is \(2\).  
The three smallest divisors are at least \(2,3,4\) (if \(4\mid a_1\)) or \(2,3,5\) etc.  
In any case  
\[
\frac1{d_1}+\frac1{d_2}+\frac1{d_3}\le\frac12+\frac14+\frac15=\frac{19}{20}<1,
\]
hence \(f(a_1)<a_1\).

Thus for any \(N\) not divisible by \(6\) we have \(f(N)<N\) whenever \(N\) has at least three proper divisors.  
If \(a_1\) were not a multiple of \(6\) the sequence would be strictly decreasing and eventually reach a number with fewer than three proper divisors (a prime or a square of a prime), contradicting the hypothesis that every term has at least three proper divisors.  
Therefore \(a_1\) is a multiple of \(6\).

---

### 2.  Notation and basic facts

Write \(a_1=6k\).  
Let  
\[
a=v_2(a_1),\qquad b=v_3(a_1),\qquad a_1=2^a3^b u,
\]
where \(a\ge1,\;b\ge1\) and \(u\) is odd, \(3\nmid u\).

---

### 3.  The case \(a=1\)

If \(a=1\) then \(a_1=2\cdot3^b u\).  
The three smallest divisors \(>1\) are \(2,3,6\) provided \(5\nmid u\); then \(f(a_1)=a_1\) (fixed point).  
If \(5\mid u\) then the third smallest divisor is \(5\), so  
\[
f(a_1)=\frac{31}{30}a_1,
\]
which is odd and divisible by \(3\).  An odd number not divisible by \(6\) satisfies \(f(M)<M\) as argued in §1, so the sequence would strictly decrease and eventually become a prime or a square of a prime, impossible.  
Hence for an infinite sequence we must have \(a=1\) and \(5\nmid u\).

---

### 4.  The case \(a\ge2\)

Now \(a\ge2\) so \(a_1\) is divisible by \(4\).  The three smallest divisors are \(2,3,4\) (because \(4<6\) and \(3<4\)), therefore  
\[
f(a_1)=\frac{13}{12}a_1.
\]
Moreover,  
\[
v_2\bigl(f(a_1)\bigr)=a-2,\qquad v_3\bigl(f(a_1)\bigr)=b-1.
\]

We claim that if \(a\) is even then the sequence cannot be infinite.  
Assume \(a\) is even and \(a\ge2\).  Then \(a-2\) is also even.  Iterating the recurrence as long as the exponent of \(2\) is at least \(2\) and the exponent of \(3\) is at least \(1\), we obtain numbers  
\[
N_t=13^t\,2^{a-2t}\,3^{b-t}\,u,\qquad t=0,1,\dots,b.
\]
When \(t=b\) we get  
\[
N_b=13^b\,2^{a-2b}\,u.
\]
Since \(a\) is even, \(a-2b\) is even.  
If \(a-2b>0\) then \(N_b\) is even and not divisible by \(3\); if \(a-2b=0\) then \(N_b\) is odd and not divisible by \(3\).  
In either case \(N_b\) is not a multiple of \(6\), so from that point onward the sequence would strictly decrease (as in §1) and eventually hit a prime or a square of a prime, contradicting the requirement.  
Thus \(a\) cannot be even; it must be odd.

---

### 5.  Necessary conditions when \(a\ge3\) is odd

Let \(a\) be odd and \(a\ge3\).  Consider the numbers  
\[
N_t=13^t\,2^{a-2t}\,3^{b-t}\,u,\qquad t=0,1,\dots,\frac{a-1}{2}.
\]
For \(t<\frac{a-1}{2}\) we have \(a-2t\ge2\) and \(b-t\ge1\) (since otherwise we would be in a situation that forces a decrease; see below).  Hence the three smallest divisors of \(N_t\) are \(2,3,4\), so  
\[
f(N_t)=13\,2^{a-2(t+1)}\,3^{b-(t+1)}\,u=N_{t+1}.
\]
The process continues until we reach \(t=\frac{a-1}{2}\).  At that step the number is  
\[
N=\bigl(13^{(a-1)/2}\bigr)\,2\,3^{b-(a-1)/2}\,u.
\]
For the sequence to stay within numbers having at least three proper divisors we must have that \(N\) itself does not lead to a decrease.  This requires that when \(v_2=1\) the number still be divisible by \(3\) (so that the triple is \(2,3,6\) and the function becomes constant) and that the third smallest divisor is \(6\) (not \(5\)).  

* The exponent of \(3\) in \(N\) is \(b-\frac{a-1}{2}\).  To have a fixed point we need \(b-\frac{a-1}{2}\ge1\), i.e.  
\[
b\ge\frac{a-1}{2}+1.
\]

* If \(5\mid u\) then the third smallest divisor of \(N\) would be \(5\) (since \(5<6\)), so \(f(N)<N\) and the process would then decrease, as before.  Hence we must have \(5\nmid u\).

Therefore the necessary conditions are:  
\(a\) is odd, \(b\ge\frac{a-1}{2}+1\), and \(5\nmid u\).

---

### 6.  Sufficiency of the conditions

Suppose \(a_1\) satisfies  
\(a=v_2(a_1)\) odd, \(b=v_3(a_1)\ge\frac{a-1}{2}+1\), and \(5\nmid u\) in the decomposition \(a_1=2^a3^b u\) with \(3\nmid u\).  

If \(a=1\) then \(a_1\) is a fixed point (since \(5\nmid u\)), and the sequence is constant, every term having at least three proper divisors.  

If \(a\ge3\) odd, consider the numbers  
\[
N_t=13^t\,2^{a-2t}\,3^{b-t}\,u,\qquad 0\le t\le\frac{a-1}{2}.
\]
For \(t<\frac{a-1}{2}\) we have \(a-2t\ge2\) and \(b-t\ge1\); the three smallest divisors are \(2,3,4\), so \(f(N_t)=N_{t+1}\).  
At \(t=\frac{a-1}{2}\) we obtain  
\[
N=2\cdot3^{b-\frac{a-1}{2}}\cdot13^{\frac{a-1}{2}}\cdot u,
\]
with \(b-\frac{a-1}{2}\ge1\) and \(5\nmid u\).  The three smallest divisors of \(N\) are \(2,3,6\); consequently \(f(N)=N\).  
Thus the sequence increases strictly until it becomes constant at this fixed point.  Every term is a multiple of \(6\) (hence has at least the proper divisors \(1,2,3\)), and none is a prime or a square of a prime.  Therefore the sequence is defined for all \(n\) and satisfies the required conditions.

---

### 7.  Description of all possible \(a_1\)

Consequently, the admissible values of \(a_1\) are exactly the positive integers \(N\) that are **not** prime and **not** a square of a prime, and such that when we write  
\(N=2^a3^b t\) with \(t\) odd and \(3\nmid t\) (so that \(a=v_2(N)\), \(b=v_3(N)\)), we have  

* \(a\) odd,  
* \(b\ge\frac{a-1}{2}+1\),  
* \(5\nmid t\).

Equivalently,  
\[
\boxed{\,a_1\in\{2^a3^b t\mid a\text{ odd},\; b\ge\frac{a-1}{2}+1,\; t\text{ odd},\; 3\nmid t,\; 5\nmid t\,\}.}
\]

</solution>
<self_evaluation>
The proof is rigorous: it first shows that \(a_1\) must be divisible by 6, then treats the two cases separately, analysing the exponent of 2 and 3.  Necessity and sufficiency are both proved by examining the three smallest divisors and the iterative behaviour.  All gaps are filled with clear arguments about the sum of reciprocals and the monotonicity of the sequence.  The final description is exact.
</self_evaluation>
